<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Teenage pregnancy</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><table class="infobox" style="width:22em">

<th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold;background:#ccc">Teenage pregnancy</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Preventing_Teen_Pregnancy_in_the_US-CDC_Vital_Signs-April_2011.pdf"><img alt="Preventing Teen Pregnancy in the US-CDC Vital Signs-April 2011.pdf" data-file-height="1687" data-file-width="1312" height="386" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Preventing_Teen_Pregnancy_in_the_US-CDC_Vital_Signs-April_2011.pdf/page1-300px-Preventing_Teen_Pregnancy_in_the_US-CDC_Vital_Signs-April_2011.pdf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Preventing_Teen_Pregnancy_in_the_US-CDC_Vital_Signs-April_2011.pdf/page1-450px-Preventing_Teen_Pregnancy_in_the_US-CDC_Vital_Signs-April_2011.pdf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Preventing_Teen_Pregnancy_in_the_US-CDC_Vital_Signs-April_2011.pdf/page1-600px-Preventing_Teen_Pregnancy_in_the_US-CDC_Vital_Signs-April_2011.pdf.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a></td>
</tr>
<tr>

<tr class="noprint">
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<div style="text-align: right;">[<a class="extiw" href="https://www.wikidata.org/wiki/Q535691" title="d:Q535691">edit on Wikidata</a>]</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Teenage pregnancy</b> is <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy" title="Pregnancy">pregnancy</a> in human females under the age of 20. A girl can become pregnant from <a href="/wiki/Sexual_intercourse" title="Sexual intercourse">sexual intercourse</a> after she has begun to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ovulate" title="Ovulate">ovulate</a> which can be before her <a href="/wiki/Menarche" title="Menarche">first menstrual period</a> (menarche), but usually occurs after the onset of her periods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> In well-nourished girls, menarche usually takes place around the age of 12 or 13.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Pregnant teenagers face many of the same <a href="/wiki/Obstetrics" title="Obstetrics">obstetrics</a> issues as other women. There are, however, additional medical concerns for pregnant girls aged under 15, who are less likely to have become physically developed enough to sustain a healthy pregnancy or to give birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-savethechildren_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-savethechildren-4">[4]</a></sup> For girls aged 15–19 risks are associated more with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Socioeconomic" title="Socioeconomic">socioeconomic</a> factors than with the biological effects of age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-makinson_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-makinson-5">[5]</a></sup> Risks of <a href="/wiki/Low_birth_weight" title="Low birth weight">low birth weight</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Premature_labor" title="Premature labor">premature labor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anemia" title="Anemia">anemia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pre-eclampsia" title="Pre-eclampsia">pre-eclampsia</a> are connected to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_age" title="Biological age">biological age</a> itself, as it was observed in teen births even after controlling for other risk factors (such as <a href="/wiki/Utilization" title="Utilization">utilization</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antenatal_care" title="Antenatal care">antenatal care</a> etc.).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Every day in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_countries" title="Developing countries">developing countries</a>, 20,000 girls under age 18 give birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unfpa.org_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-unfpa.org-8">[8]</a></sup> This amounts to 7.3 million births a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unfpa.org_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-unfpa.org-8">[8]</a></sup> If all pregnancies are included, the number of adolescent pregnancies is much higher.</p>
<p>In developed countries, teenage pregnancies are often associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_issues" title="Social issues">social issues</a>, including lower <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Educational" title="Educational">educational</a> levels, higher rates of <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">poverty</a>, and other poorer life outcomes in children of teenage mothers. Teenage pregnancy in developed countries is usually outside of <a href="/wiki/Marriage" title="Marriage">marriage</a>, and carries a <a href="/wiki/Social_stigma" title="Social stigma">social stigma</a> in many communities and cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-9">[9]</a></sup> By contrast, teenage parents in developing countries are often married, and their pregnancies welcomed by family and society. However, in these societies, early pregnancy may combine with <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a> and poor health care to cause medical problems.</p>
<p>Teenage pregnancies appear to be preventable by comprehensive <a href="/wiki/Sex_education" title="Sex education">sex education</a> and access to <a href="/wiki/Birth_control" title="Birth control">birth control</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cochrane2009_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cochrane2009-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="Updated version https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/26839116 (June 2016)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup> <a href="/wiki/Abstinence-only_sex_education" title="Abstinence-only sex education">Abstinence-only sex education</a> does not appear to be effective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Obs07_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Obs07-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teenage_pregnancy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The age of the mother is determined by the easily verified date when the pregnancy <i>ends</i>, not by the estimated date of conception.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kost_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kost-12">[12]</a></sup> Consequently, the statistics do not include pregnancies that began in women aged 19 if they ended on or after the woman's 20th birthday.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kost_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kost-12">[12]</a></sup> Similarly, statistics on the mother's marital status are determined by whether she is married at the end of the pregnancy, not at the time of conception.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects">Effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teenage_pregnancy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Population_Fund" title="United Nations Population Fund">United Nations Population Fund</a> (UNFPA), “Pregnancies among girls less than 18 years of age have irreparable consequences. It violates the rights of girls, with life-threatening consequences in terms of sexual and reproductive health, and poses high development costs for communities, particularly in perpetuating the cycle of poverty.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adolescent_Pregnancy_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adolescent_Pregnancy-13">[13]</a></sup> Health consequences include not yet being physically ready for pregnancy and childbirth leading to complications and malnutrition as the majority of adolescents tend to come from lower-income households. The risk of maternal death for girls under age 15 in low- and middle-income countries is higher than for women in their twenties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adolescent_Pregnancy_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Adolescent_Pregnancy-13">[13]</a></sup> Teenage pregnancy also affects girls’ education and income potential as many are forced to drop out of school which ultimately threatens future opportunities and economic prospects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unfpa.org_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-unfpa.org-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Several studies have examined the <a href="/wiki/Socioeconomics" title="Socioeconomics">socioeconomic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medical</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychological</a> impact of pregnancy and parenthood in teens. Life outcomes for teenage mothers and their children vary; other factors, such as <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">poverty</a> or <a href="/wiki/Social_support" title="Social support">social support</a>, may be more important than the age of the mother at the birth. Many solutions to counteract the more negative findings have been proposed. Teenage parents who can rely on family and community support, social services and child-care support are more likely to continue their education and get higher paying jobs as they progress with their education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>A holistic approach is required in order to address teenage pregnancy. This means not focusing on changing the behaviour of girls but addressing the underlying reasons of adolescent pregnancy such as poverty, gender inequality, social pressures and coercion. This approach should include “providing age-appropriate comprehensive sexuality education for all young people, investing in girls’ education, preventing child marriage, sexual violence and coercion, building gender-equitable societies by empowering girls and engaging men and boys and ensuring adolescents’ access to sexual and reproductive health information as well as services that welcome them and facilitate their choices.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unfpa.org_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-unfpa.org-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mother">Mother</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teenage_pregnancy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Mother">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Teen_birth_rates_internationally,_per_1000_girls_aged_15-19_2008.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2554" data-file-width="4578" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Teen_birth_rates_internationally%2C_per_1000_girls_aged_15-19_2008.png/220px-Teen_birth_rates_internationally%2C_per_1000_girls_aged_15-19_2008.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Teen_birth_rates_internationally%2C_per_1000_girls_aged_15-19_2008.png/330px-Teen_birth_rates_internationally%2C_per_1000_girls_aged_15-19_2008.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Teen_birth_rates_internationally%2C_per_1000_girls_aged_15-19_2008.png/440px-Teen_birth_rates_internationally%2C_per_1000_girls_aged_15-19_2008.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Teen_birth_rates_internationally,_per_1000_girls_aged_15-19_2008.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
In 2008 teen birth rates internationally, per 1000 girls aged 15–19. United States and United Kingdom had some of the highest teenage pregnancy rates in the developed world.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Being a <i>young mother</i> in a first world country can affect one's <a href="/wiki/Education" title="Education">education</a>. Teen mothers are more likely to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dropping_out" title="Dropping out">drop out</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_school" title="High school">high school</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natcamp_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-natcamp-15">[15]</a></sup> However, recent studies have found that many of these mothers had already dropped out of school before becoming pregnant, but those in school at the time of their pregnancy were as likely to graduate as their peers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> One study in 2001 found that women who gave birth during their teens completed <a href="/wiki/Secondary_education" title="Secondary education">secondary-level schooling</a> 10–12% as often and pursued <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post-secondary_education" title="Post-secondary education">post-secondary education</a> 14–29% as often as women who waited until age 30.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> <i>Young motherhood</i> in an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrialized_country" title="Industrialized country">industrialized country</a> can affect <a href="/wiki/Employment" title="Employment">employment</a> and <a href="/wiki/Social_class" title="Social class">social class</a>. Less than one third of teenage mothers receive any form of child support, vastly increasing the likelihood of turning to the government for assistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> The correlation between earlier childbearing and failure to complete high school reduces career opportunities for many young women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natcamp_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-natcamp-15">[15]</a></sup> One study found that, in 1988, 60% of teenage mothers were <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">impoverished</a> at the time of giving birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Additional research found that nearly 50% of all adolescent mothers sought <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Welfare_(financial_aid)" title="Welfare (financial aid)">social assistance</a> within the first five years of their child's life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natcamp_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-natcamp-15">[15]</a></sup> A study of 100 teenaged mothers in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> found that only 11% received a <a href="/wiki/Salary" title="Salary">salary</a>, while the remaining 89% were <a href="/wiki/Unemployment" title="Unemployment">unemployed</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Most British teenage mothers live in <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">poverty</a>, with nearly half in the bottom fifth of the income distribution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dfes_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-dfes-21">[21]</a></sup> Teenage women who are pregnant or mothers are seven times more likely to commit suicide than other teenagers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Professor John Ermisch at the institute of social and economic research at Essex University and Dr Roger Ingham, director of the centre of sexual health at Southampton University – found that comparing teenage mothers with other girls with similarly deprived social-economic profiles, bad school experiences and low educational aspirations, the difference in their respective life chances was negligible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cambios_en_una_adolescente.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="4912" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Cambios_en_una_adolescente.JPG/220px-Cambios_en_una_adolescente.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Cambios_en_una_adolescente.JPG/330px-Cambios_en_una_adolescente.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Cambios_en_una_adolescente.JPG/440px-Cambios_en_una_adolescente.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cambios_en_una_adolescente.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Silouette of a pregnant teen</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Teenage motherhood may actually make economic sense for young women with less money, some research suggests. For instance, long-term studies by <a href="/wiki/Duke_University" title="Duke University">Duke University</a> economist V. Joseph Hotz and colleagues, published in 2005, found that by age 35, former teen moms had earned more in income, paid more in taxes, were substantially less likely to live in poverty and collected less in public assistance than similarly poor women who waited until their 20s to have babies. Women who became mothers in their teens — freed from child-raising duties by their late 20s and early 30s to pursue employment while poorer women who waited to become moms were still stuck at home watching their young children — wound up paying more in taxes than they had collected in welfare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Males_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Males-24">[24]</a></sup> Eight years earlier, the federally commissioned report "Kids Having Kids" also contained a similar finding, though it was buried: "Adolescent childbearers fare slightly better than later-childbearing counterparts in terms of their overall economic welfare."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Males_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Males-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the National Campaign to Prevent Teen Pregnancy, nearly 1 in 4 teen mothers will experience another pregnancy within two years of having their first.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Statistics_on_Teen_Pregnancy_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Statistics_on_Teen_Pregnancy-25">[25]</a></sup> Pregnancy and giving birth significantly increases the chance that these mothers will become high school dropouts and as many as half have to go on welfare. Many teen parents do not have the intellectual or emotional maturity that is needed to provide for another life.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Often, these pregnancies are hidden for months resulting in a lack of adequate prenatal care and dangerous outcomes for the babies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Day_2009_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Day_2009-26">[26]</a></sup> Factors that determine which mothers are more likely to have a closely spaced repeat birth include marriage and education: the likelihood decreases with the level of education of the young woman – or her parents – and increases if she gets married.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Child">Child</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teenage_pregnancy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Child">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Early motherhood can affect the <a href="/wiki/Developmental_psychology" title="Developmental psychology">psychosocial development</a> of the infant. The children of teen mothers are more likely to be born prematurely with a low birth weight, predisposing them to many other lifelong conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gibbs-2012_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gibbs-2012-28">[28]</a></sup> Children of teen mothers are at higher risk of intellectual, language, and socio-emotional delays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Day_2009_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Day_2009-26">[26]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Developmental_disability" title="Developmental disability">Developmental disabilities</a> and <a href="/wiki/Behavior" title="Behavior">behavioral</a> issues are increased in children born to teen mothers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aap_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-aap-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> One study suggested that adolescent mothers are less likely to <a href="/wiki/Stimulation" title="Stimulation">stimulate</a> their infant through <a href="/wiki/Affection" title="Affection">affectionate behaviors</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Haptic_communication" title="Haptic communication">touch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smile" title="Smile">smiling</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">verbal communication</a>, or to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sensitivity_(human)" title="Sensitivity (human)">sensitive</a> and <a href="/wiki/Acceptance" title="Acceptance">accepting</a> toward his or her needs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aap_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-aap-29">[29]</a></sup> Another found that those who had more <a href="/wiki/Social_support" title="Social support">social support</a> were less likely to show <a href="/wiki/Anger" title="Anger">anger</a> toward their children or to rely upon <a href="/wiki/Punishment" title="Punishment">punishment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>Poor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grade_(education)" title="Grade (education)">academic performance</a> in the children of teenage mothers has also been noted, with many of the children being held back a grade level, scoring lower on standardized tests, and/or failing to graduate from secondary school.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natcamp_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-natcamp-15">[15]</a></sup> Daughters born to adolescent <a href="/wiki/Parent" title="Parent">parents</a> are more likely to become teen mothers themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natcamp_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-natcamp-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Furstenberg_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Furstenberg-32">[32]</a></sup> Sons born to teenage mothers are three times more likely to <a href="/wiki/Sentence_(law)" title="Sentence (law)">serve time</a> in <a href="/wiki/Prison" title="Prison">prison</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical">Medical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teenage_pregnancy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Medical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Maternal and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prenatal" title="Prenatal">prenatal</a> health is of particular concern among teens who are pregnant or parenting. The worldwide incidence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Premature_birth" title="Premature birth">premature birth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Birth_weight" title="Birth weight">low birth weight</a> is higher among adolescent mothers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-makinson_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-makinson-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natcamp_15-5"><a href="#cite_note-natcamp-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> In a rural hospital in West Bengal, teenage mothers between 15 and 19 years old were more likely to have anemia, preterm delivery, and a baby with a lower birth weight than mothers between 20 and 24 years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>Research indicates that pregnant teens are less likely to receive <a href="/wiki/Prenatal_care" title="Prenatal care">prenatal care</a>, often seeking it in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Third_trimester" title="Third trimester">third trimester</a>, if at all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-makinson_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-makinson-5">[5]</a></sup> The Guttmacher Institute reports that one-third of pregnant teens receive insufficient prenatal care and that their children are more likely to have health issues in childhood or be <a href="/wiki/Inpatient_care" title="Inpatient care">hospitalized</a> than those born to older women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Young mothers who are given high-quality maternity care have significantly healthier babies than those who do not. Many of the health-issues associated with teenage mothers appear to result from lack of access to adequate medical care.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many pregnant teens are at risk of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Illnesses_related_to_poor_nutrition" title="Illnesses related to poor nutrition">nutritional deficiencies</a> from poor <a href="/wiki/Diet_(nutrition)" title="Diet (nutrition)">eating habits</a> common in adolescence, including attempts to <a href="/wiki/Weight_loss" title="Weight loss">lose weight</a> through <a href="/wiki/Dieting" title="Dieting">dieting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fasting" title="Fasting">skipping meals</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_faddism" title="Food faddism">food faddism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Snack_food" title="Snack food">snacking</a>, and consumption of <a href="/wiki/Fast_food" title="Fast food">fast food</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>Inadequate <a href="/wiki/Nutrition_and_pregnancy" title="Nutrition and pregnancy">nutrition during pregnancy</a> is an even more marked problem among teenagers in <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">developing countries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Complications_of_pregnancy" title="Complications of pregnancy">Complications of pregnancy</a> result in the deaths of an estimated 70,000 teen girls in developing countries each year. Young mothers and their babies are also at greater risk of contracting <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-savethechildren_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-savethechildren-4">[4]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> estimates that the risk of death following pregnancy is twice as high for women aged 15–19 than for those aged 20–24. The maternal mortality rate can be up to five times higher for girls aged 10–14 than for women aged 20–24. Illegal abortion also holds many risks for teenage girls in areas such as sub-Saharan Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-subsahara_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-subsahara-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Risks for medical complications are greater for girls aged under 15, as an underdeveloped <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_pelvis" title="Human pelvis">pelvis</a> can lead to difficulties in <a href="/wiki/Childbirth" title="Childbirth">childbirth</a>. Obstructed labour is normally dealt with by <a href="/wiki/Caesarean_section" title="Caesarean section">Caesarean section</a> in <a href="/wiki/Developed_country" title="Developed country">industrialized nations</a>; however, in developing regions where medical services might be unavailable, it can lead to <a href="/wiki/Eclampsia" title="Eclampsia">eclampsia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Obstetric_fistula" title="Obstetric fistula">obstetric fistula</a>, <a href="/wiki/Infant_mortality" title="Infant mortality">infant mortality</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Maternal_death" title="Maternal death">maternal death</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-savethechildren_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-savethechildren-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unfpa.org_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-unfpa.org-8">[8]</a></sup> For mothers who are older than fifteen, age in itself is not a risk factor, and poor outcomes are associated more with socioeconomic factors rather than with biology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-makinson_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-makinson-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Risk_factors">Risk factors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teenage_pregnancy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Risk factors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teenage_pregnancy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Rates of teenage pregnancies are higher in societies where it is traditional for girls to marry young and where they are encouraged to bear children as soon as they are able. For example, in some sub-Saharan African countries, early pregnancy is often seen as a blessing because it is proof of the young woman's fertility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-subsahara_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-subsahara-41">[41]</a></sup> Countries where teenage marriages are common experience higher levels of teenage pregnancies. In the Indian subcontinent, early marriage and pregnancy is more common in traditional rural communities than in cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-escap_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-escap-42">[42]</a></sup> The lack of education on safe sex, whether it is from parents, schools, or otherwise, is a cause of teenage pregnancy. Many teenagers are not taught about methods of birth control and how to deal with peers who pressure them into having sex before they are ready. Many pregnant teenagers do not have any cognition of the central facts of sexuality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>Economic incentives also influence the decision to have children. In societies where children are set to work at an early age it is economically attractive to have many children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>In societies where adolescent marriage is less common, such as many developed countries, young age at first <a href="/wiki/Sexual_intercourse" title="Sexual intercourse">intercourse</a> and lack of use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Contraceptive" title="Contraceptive">contraceptive</a> methods (or their inconsistent and/or incorrect use; the use of a method with a high failure rate is also a problem) may be factors in teen pregnancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oecd_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-oecd-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toosoon_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-toosoon-46">[46]</a></sup> Most teenage pregnancies in the developed world appear to be unplanned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toosoon_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-toosoon-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psi_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-psi-47">[47]</a></sup> Many Western countries have instituted <a href="/wiki/Sex_education" title="Sex education">sex education</a> programs, the main objective of which is to reduce unplanned pregnancies and STDs. Countries with low levels of teenagers giving birth accept sexual relationships among teenagers and provide comprehensive and balanced information about sexuality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guttmacher2_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-guttmacher2-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_family_members">Other family members</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teenage_pregnancy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Other family members">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Teen pregnancy and motherhood can influence younger siblings. One study found that the younger sisters of teen mothers were less likely to emphasize the importance of <a href="/wiki/Education" title="Education">education</a> and <a href="/wiki/Employment" title="Employment">employment</a> and more likely to accept <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_sexual_behavior" title="Human sexual behavior">human sexual behavior</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parenting" title="Parenting">parenting</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Marriage" title="Marriage">marriage</a> at younger ages; younger brothers, too, were found to be more tolerant of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Illegitimacy" title="Illegitimacy">non-marital</a> and early births, in addition to being more susceptible to <a href="/wiki/Recklessness_(psychology)" title="Recklessness (psychology)">high-risk behaviors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> If the younger sisters of teenage parents babysit the children, they have an increased risk of getting pregnant themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobson_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobson-50">[50]</a></sup> Once an older daughter has a child, parents often become more accepting as time goes by.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The probability of the younger sister having a teenage pregnancy went from one in five to two in five if the elder sister had a baby as a teenager.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexuality">Sexuality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teenage_pregnancy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Sexuality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Adolescent_sexuality" title="Adolescent sexuality">Adolescent sexuality</a></div>
<p>In most countries, most males experience sexual intercourse for the first time before their 20th birthdays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GImen_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-GImen-53">[53]</a></sup> Males in Western developed countries have sex for the first time sooner than in undeveloped and culturally conservative countries such as Sub-Saharan Africa and much of Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GImen_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-GImen-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a 2005 <a href="/wiki/Kaiser_Family_Foundation" title="Kaiser Family Foundation">Kaiser Family Foundation</a> study of US teenagers, 29% of teens reported feeling pressure to have sex, 33% of sexually active teens reported "being in a relationship where they felt things were moving too fast sexually", and 24% had "done something sexual they didn’t really want to do".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kaiser_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-kaiser-54">[54]</a></sup> Several polls have indicated <a href="/wiki/Peer_pressure" title="Peer pressure">peer pressure</a> as a factor in encouraging both girls and boys to have sex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pollingdata_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-pollingdata-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psychologytoday_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-psychologytoday-56">[56]</a></sup> The increased sexual activity among adolescents is manifested in increased teenage pregnancies and an increase in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sexually_transmitted_diseases" title="Sexually transmitted diseases">sexually transmitted diseases</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_of_drug_and_alcohol_use">Role of drug and alcohol use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teenage_pregnancy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Role of drug and alcohol use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Inhibition-reducing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drug_abuse" title="Drug abuse">drugs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohol</a> may possibly encourage unintended sexual activity. If so, it is unknown if the drugs themselves directly influence teenagers to engage in riskier behavior, or whether teenagers who engage in drug use are more likely to engage in sex. <a href="/wiki/Correlation_does_not_imply_causation" title="Correlation does not imply causation">Correlation does not imply causation</a>. The drugs with the strongest evidence linking them to teenage pregnancy are <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cannabis_(drug)" title="Cannabis (drug)">cannabis</a>, <a href="/wiki/MDMA" title="MDMA">"ecstasy"</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Substituted_amphetamine" title="Substituted amphetamine">substituted amphetamines</a>. The drugs with the least evidence to support a link to early pregnancy are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Opioids" title="Opioids">opioids</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Heroin" title="Heroin">heroin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Morphine" title="Morphine">morphine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oxycodone" title="Oxycodone">oxycodone</a>, of which a well-known effect is the significant reduction of <a href="/wiki/Libido" title="Libido">libido</a> – it appears that teenage <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Opioid_dependence" title="Opioid dependence">opioid</a> <a href="/wiki/Recreational_drug_use" title="Recreational drug use">users</a> have significantly reduced rates of conception compared to their non-using, and <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohol</a>, <a href="/wiki/MDMA" title="MDMA">"ecstasy"</a>, cannabis, and <a href="/wiki/Amphetamine" title="Amphetamine">amphetamine</a> using peers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oecd_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-oecd-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kaiser_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-kaiser-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Besharov_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Besharov-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_puberty">Early puberty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teenage_pregnancy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Early puberty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Precocious_puberty" title="Precocious puberty">Precocious puberty</a></div>
<p>Girls who mature early are more likely to engage in sexual intercourse at a younger age, which in turn puts them at greater risk of teenage pregnancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lack_of_contraception">Lack of contraception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teenage_pregnancy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Lack of contraception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Birth_control" title="Birth control">Birth control</a></div>
<p>Adolescents may lack knowledge of, or access to, conventional methods of preventing pregnancy, as they may be too embarrassed or frightened to seek such information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pollingdata_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-pollingdata-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britsexed_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-britsexed-60">[60]</a></sup> Contraception for teenagers presents a huge challenge for the clinician. In 1998, the government of the United Kingdom set a target to halve the under-18 pregnancy rate by 2010. The Teenage Pregnancy Strategy (TPS) was established to achieve this. The pregnancy rate in this group, although falling, rose slightly in 2007, to 41.7 per 1000 women. Young women often think of contraception either as 'the pill' or condoms and have little knowledge about other methods. They are heavily influenced by negative, second-hand stories about methods of contraception from their friends and the media. Prejudices are extremely difficult to overcome. Over concern about side-effects, for example <a href="/wiki/Weight_gain" title="Weight gain">weight gain</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acne" title="Acne">acne</a>, often affect choice. Missing up to three pills a month is common, and in this age group the figure is likely to be higher. Restarting after the pill-free week, having to hide pills, drug interactions and difficulty getting repeat prescriptions can all lead to method failure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the United States, according to the 2002 National Surveys of Family Growth, sexually active adolescent women wishing to avoid pregnancy were less likely than older women to use contraceptives (18% of 15–19-year-olds used no contraceptives, versus 10.7% for women aged 15–44).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trusell.26Wynn_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-trusell.26Wynn-62">[62]</a></sup> More than 80% of teen pregnancies are unintended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-speidel_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-speidel-63">[63]</a></sup> Over half of <a href="/wiki/Unintended_pregnancy" title="Unintended pregnancy">unintended pregnancies</a> were to women not using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Contraceptives" title="Contraceptives">contraceptives</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trusell.26Wynn_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-trusell.26Wynn-62">[62]</a></sup> most of the rest are due to inconsistent or incorrect use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-speidel_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-speidel-63">[63]</a></sup> 23% of sexually active young women in a 1996 <i>Seventeen</i> magazine poll admitted to having had <a href="/wiki/Safe_sex" title="Safe sex">unprotected sex</a> with a partner who did not use a condom, while 70% of girls in a 1997 <i><a href="/wiki/Parade_(magazine)" title="Parade (magazine)">PARADE</a></i> poll claimed it was embarrassing to buy birth control or request information from a doctor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pollingdata_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-pollingdata-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a 2012 study, over 1,000 females were surveyed to find out factors contributing to not using contraception. Of those surveyed, almost half had been involved in unprotected sex within the previous three months. These women gave three main reasons for not using contraceptives: trouble obtaining birth control (the most frequent reason), lack of intention to have sex, and the misconception that they "could not get pregnant."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a study for The Guttmacher Institute, researchers found that from a comparative perspective, however, teenage pregnancy rates in the United States are less nuanced than one might initially assume. “Since timing and levels of sexual activity are quite similar across [Sweden, France, Canada, Great Britain, and the U.S.], the high U.S. rates arise primarily because of less, and possibly less-effective, contraceptive use by sexually active teenagers.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DJE_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-DJE-65">[65]</a></sup> Thus, the cause for the discrepancy between rich nations can be traced largely to contraceptive-based issues.</p>
<p>Among teens in the UK seeking an abortion, a study found that the rate of contraceptive use was roughly the same for teens as for older women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>In other cases, contraception is used, but proves to be inadequate. Inexperienced adolescents may use <a href="/wiki/Condom" title="Condom">condoms</a> incorrectly, forget to take <a href="/wiki/Combined_oral_contraceptive_pill" title="Combined oral contraceptive pill">oral contraceptives</a>, or fail to use the contraceptives they had previously chosen. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pearl_index" title="Pearl index">Contraceptive failure rates</a> are higher for teenagers, particularly poor ones, than for older users.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Besharov_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Besharov-57">[57]</a></sup> Long-acting contraceptives such as <a href="/wiki/Intrauterine_device" title="Intrauterine device">intrauterine devices</a>, subcutaneous <a href="/wiki/Contraceptive_implant" title="Contraceptive implant">contraceptive implants</a>, and contraceptive injections (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Depo-Provera" title="Depo-Provera">Depo-Provera</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Combined_injectable_contraceptive" title="Combined injectable contraceptive">Combined injectable contraceptive</a>), which prevent pregnancy for months or years at a time, are more effective in women who have trouble remembering to take pills or using barrier methods consistently.</p>
<p>According to The Encyclopedia of Women's Health, published in 2004, there has been an increased effort to provide contraception to adolescents via family planning services and school-based health, such as HIV prevention education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Teen_Pregnancy_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Teen_Pregnancy-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Age_gap_in_relationships">Age gap in relationships</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teenage_pregnancy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Age gap in relationships">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Age_disparity_in_sexual_relationships" title="Age disparity in sexual relationships">Age disparity in sexual relationships</a></div>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Family_Research_Council" title="Family Research Council">Family Research Council</a>, a conservative lobbying organization, studies in the US indicate that the age gap between teenage girls and the men who impregnate them is an important contributing factor. Teenage girls in relationships with older males, are more likely to become pregnant than those in relationships with boys their own age. They are also more likely to carry the baby to term rather than have an abortion. A review of California's 1990 vital statistics found that men aged over 19 fathered 77% of all births to girls aged 16–18, and 51% of births to girls aged under 16. Men aged over 25 fathered twice as many children of teenage mothers as boys aged under 18, and men aged over 20 fathered five times as many children of girls aged under 16 as boys aged under 16. A 1992 Washington state study of 535 adolescent mothers found that 62% of the mothers had a history of being raped or sexually molested by men whose ages averaged 27 years. This study found that, compared with nonabused mothers, abused adolescent mothers initiated sex earlier, had sex with much older partners, and engaged in riskier, more frequent, and promiscuous sex. Studies by the <a href="/wiki/Population_Reference_Bureau" title="Population Reference Bureau">Population Reference Bureau</a> and the National Center for Health Statistics found that about two-thirds of children born to teenage girls in the United States are fathered by adult men age 20 or older.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hzu_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hzu-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexual_abuse">Sexual abuse</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teenage_pregnancy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Sexual abuse">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sexual_abuse" title="Sexual abuse">Sexual abuse</a></div>
<p>Studies from South Africa have found that 11–20% of pregnancies in teenagers are a direct result of rape, while about 60% of teenage mothers had unwanted sexual experiences preceding their pregnancy. Before age 15, a majority of first-intercourse experiences among females are reported to be non-voluntary; the Guttmacher Institute found that 60% of girls who had sex before age 15 were coerced by males who on average were six years their senior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> One in five teenage fathers admitted to forcing girls to have sex with them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>Multiple studies have indicated a strong link between early childhood sexual abuse and subsequent teenage pregnancy in industrialized countries. Up to 70% of women who gave birth in their teens were molested as young girls; by contrast, 25% of women who did not give birth as teens were molested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>In some countries, sexual intercourse between a minor and an adult is not considered consensual under the law because a minor is believed to lack the maturity and competence to make an informed decision to engage in fully consensual sex with an adult. In those countries, sex with a minor is therefore considered <a href="/wiki/Statutory_rape" title="Statutory rape">statutory rape</a>. In most European countries, by contrast, once an adolescent has reached the age of consent, he or she can legally have sexual relations with adults because it is held that in general (although certain limitations may still apply), reaching the age of consent enables a juvenile to consent to sex with any partner who has also reached that age. Therefore, the definition of statutory rape is limited to sex with a person under the minimum age of consent. What constitutes statutory rape ultimately differs by jurisdiction (see <a href="/wiki/Age_of_consent" title="Age of consent">age of consent</a>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dating_violence">Dating violence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teenage_pregnancy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Dating violence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Dating_abuse" title="Dating abuse">Dating abuse</a> and <a href="/wiki/Teen_dating_violence" title="Teen dating violence">Teen dating violence</a></div>
<p>Studies have indicated that adolescent girls are often in abusive relationships at the time of their conceiving.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> They have also reported that knowledge of their pregnancy has often intensified violent and controlling behaviors on part of their boyfriends. Girls under age 18 are twice as likely to be beaten by their child's father than women over age 18. A UK study found that 70% of women who gave birth in their teens had experienced adolescent domestic violence. Similar results have been found in studies in the United States. A Washington State study found 70% of teenage mothers had been beaten by their boyfriends, 51% had experienced attempts of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birth_control_sabotage" title="Birth control sabotage">birth control sabotage</a> within the last year, and 21% experienced school or work sabotage.</p>
<p>In a study of 379 pregnant or parenting teens and 95 teenage girls without children, 62% of girls aged 11–15 and 56% of girls aged 16–19 reported experiencing domestic violence at the hands of their partners. Moreover, 51% of the girls reported experiencing at least one instance where their boyfriend attempted to sabotage their efforts to use birth control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Socioeconomic_factors">Socioeconomic factors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teenage_pregnancy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Socioeconomic factors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Child_Mother.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="749" data-file-width="510" height="323" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/The_Child_Mother.jpg/220px-The_Child_Mother.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/The_Child_Mother.jpg/330px-The_Child_Mother.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/The_Child_Mother.jpg/440px-The_Child_Mother.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Child_Mother.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A young poverty-stricken girl clutches her child. Frontispiece illustration from <i>Street Arabs and Gutter Snipes</i> by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=George_Carter_Needham&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="George Carter Needham (page does not exist)">George Carter Needham</a>, Boston, 1884.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Teenage pregnancy has been defined predominantly within the research field and among social agencies as a social problem. <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">Poverty</a> is associated with increased rates of teenage pregnancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Besharov_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-Besharov-57">[57]</a></sup> Economically poor countries such as <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a> have far more teenage mothers compared with economically rich countries such as <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-globalis_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-globalis-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the UK, around half of all pregnancies to under 18s are concentrated among the 30% most deprived population, with only 14% occurring among the 30% least deprived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> For example, in <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, the teenage birth rate in the well-off <a href="/wiki/Central_Italy" title="Central Italy">central regions</a> is only 3.3 per 1,000, while in the poorer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mezzogiorno" title="Mezzogiorno">Mezzogiorno</a> it is 10.0 per 1,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oecd_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-oecd-45">[45]</a></sup> Similarly, in the United States, sociologist <a href="/wiki/Mike_A._Males" title="Mike A. Males">Mike A. Males</a> noted that teenage birth rates closely mapped poverty rates in <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tr>
<th>County</th>
<th>Poverty rate</th>
<th>Birth rate*</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Marin_County,_California" title="Marin County, California">Marin County</a></td>
<td>5%</td>
<td>5</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Tulare_County,_California" title="Tulare County, California">Tulare County</a> (Caucasians)</td>
<td>18%</td>
<td>50</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Tulare_County,_California" title="Tulare County, California">Tulare County</a> (Hispanics)</td>
<td>40%</td>
<td>100</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><small>* per 1000 women aged 15–19</small></p>
<p>Teen pregnancy cost the United States over $9.1 billion in 2004, including $1.9 billion for health care, $2.3 billion for child welfare, $2.1 billion for incarceration, and $2.9 billion in lower tax revenue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>There is little evidence to support the common belief that teenage mothers become pregnant to get benefits, welfare, and council housing. Most knew little about housing or financial aid before they got pregnant and what they thought they knew often turned out to be wrong.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psi_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-psi-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Childhood_environment">Childhood environment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teenage_pregnancy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Childhood environment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Women exposed to abuse, <a href="/wiki/Domestic_violence" title="Domestic violence">domestic violence</a>, and family strife in childhood are more likely to become pregnant as teenagers, and the risk of becoming pregnant as a teenager increases with the number of adverse childhood experiences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> According to a 2004 study, one-third of teenage pregnancies could be prevented by eliminating exposure to abuse, violence, and family strife. The researchers note that "family dysfunction has enduring and unfavorable health consequences for women during the adolescent years, the childbearing years, and beyond." When the family environment does not include adverse childhood experiences, becoming pregnant as an adolescent does not appear to raise the likelihood of long-term, negative psychosocial consequences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Studies have also found that boys raised in homes with a battered mother, or who experienced physical violence directly, were significantly more likely to impregnate a girl.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>Studies have also found that girls whose fathers left the family early in their lives had the highest rates of early sexual activity and adolescent pregnancy. Girls whose fathers left them at a later age had a lower rate of early sexual activity, and the lowest rates are found in girls whose fathers were present throughout their childhood. Even when the researchers took into account other factors that could have contributed to early sexual activity and pregnancy, such as behavioral problems and life adversity, early father-absent girls were still about five times more likely in the United States and three times more likely in New Zealand to become pregnant as adolescents than were father-present girls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>Low <a href="/wiki/Education" title="Education">educational</a> expectations have been pinpointed as a risk factor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> A girl is also more likely to become a teenage parent if her mother or older sister gave birth in her teens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Furstenberg_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Furstenberg-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobson_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobson-50">[50]</a></sup> A majority of respondents in a 1988 <a href="/wiki/Joint_Center_for_Political_and_Economic_Studies" title="Joint Center for Political and Economic Studies">Joint Center for Political and Economic Studies</a> survey attributed the occurrence of adolescent pregnancy to a breakdown of <a href="/wiki/Interpersonal_communication" title="Interpersonal communication">communication</a> between parents and child and also to inadequate <a href="/wiki/Parental_supervision" title="Parental supervision">parental supervision</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pollingdata_55-3"><a href="#cite_note-pollingdata-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Foster_care" title="Foster care">Foster care</a> youth are more likely than their peers to become pregnant as teenagers. The National Casey Alumni Study, which surveyed foster care alumni from 23 communities across the United States, found the birth rate for girls in foster care was more than double the rate of their peers outside the foster care system. A University of Chicago study of youth transitioning out of foster care in Illinois, Iowa, and Wisconsin found that nearly half of the females had been pregnant by age 19. The Utah Department of Human Services found that girls who had left the foster care system between 1999 and 2004 had a birth rate nearly 3 times the rate for girls in the general population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media_influence">Media influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teenage_pregnancy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Media influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A study conducted in 2006 found that adolescents who were more exposed to sexuality in the media were also more likely to engage in sexual activity themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i>, "teens exposed to the most sexual content on TV are twice as likely as teens watching less of this material to become pregnant before they reach age 20".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention">Prevention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teenage_pregnancy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Prevention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Comprehensive <a href="/wiki/Sex_education" title="Sex education">sex education</a> and access to <a href="/wiki/Birth_control" title="Birth control">birth control</a> appear to reduce unplanned teenage pregnancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cochrane2009_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cochrane2009-10">[10]</a></sup> It is unclear if a single intervention is most effective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cochrane2009_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cochrane2009-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the United States free access to a <a href="/wiki/Long-acting_reversible_contraception" title="Long-acting reversible contraception">long acting form of reversible birth control</a> along with education decreased the rates of teen pregnancies by around 80% and the rate of abortions by more than 75%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teenage_pregnancy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Dutch approach to preventing teenage pregnancy has often been seen as a model by other countries. The curriculum focuses on values, attitudes, communication and negotiation skills, as well as biological aspects of reproduction. The media has encouraged open dialogue and the health-care system guarantees confidentiality and a non-judgmental approach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dutchmodel_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-dutchmodel-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Abstinence_only_education">Abstinence only education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teenage_pregnancy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Abstinence only education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:No_Sex_Signage_in_Ghana.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/No_Sex_Signage_in_Ghana.jpg/220px-No_Sex_Signage_in_Ghana.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/No_Sex_Signage_in_Ghana.jpg/330px-No_Sex_Signage_in_Ghana.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/No_Sex_Signage_in_Ghana.jpg/440px-No_Sex_Signage_in_Ghana.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:No_Sex_Signage_in_Ghana.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Add promoting abstinence in <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a>: <i>No Sex Ad</i> (Anti-<a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS" title="HIV/AIDS">HIV/AIDS</a> ― <a href="/wiki/Signage" title="Signage">Signage</a>)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Some schools provide <a href="/wiki/Abstinence-only_sex_education" title="Abstinence-only sex education">abstinence-only sex education</a>. Evidence does not support the effectiveness of abstinence-only sex education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Obs07_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Obs07-11">[11]</a></sup> It has been found to be ineffective in decreasing <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a> risk in the developed world,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Underhill2007_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Underhill2007-92">[92]</a></sup> and does not decrease rates of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unplanned_pregnancy" title="Unplanned pregnancy">unplanned pregnancy</a> when compared to comprehensive sex education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Obs07_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-Obs07-11">[11]</a></sup> It does not decrease the sexual activity rates of students, when compared to students who undertake comprehensive sexual education classes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kohler08_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kohler08-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_policy">Public policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teenage_pregnancy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Public policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the U.S., one policy initiative that has been used to increase rates of contraceptive use is Title X: Title X of the 1970 Public Health Service act provides family planning services for those who do not qualify for Medicaid by distributing "funding to a network of public, private, and nonprofit entities [to provide] services on a sliding scale based on income."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Studies indicate that, internationally, success in reducing teen pregnancy rates is directly correlated with the kind of access that Title X provides: “What appears crucial to success is that adolescents know where they can go to obtain information and services, can get there easily and are assured of receiving confidential, nonjudgmental care, and that these services and contraceptive supplies are free or cost very little.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DJE_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-DJE-65">[65]</a></sup> In addressing high rates of unplanned teen pregnancies, scholars agree that the problem must be confronted from both the biological and cultural contexts.</p>
<p>On September 30, 2010, The U.S. Department of Health and Human Services approved $155 million in new funding for comprehensive sex education programs designed to prevent teenage pregnancy. The money is being awarded "to states, non-profit organizations, school districts, universities and others. These grants will support the replication of teen pregnancy prevention programs that have been shown to be effective through rigorous research as well as the testing of new, innovative approaches to combating teen pregnancy."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Of the total of $150 million, $55 million is funded by Affordable Care Act through the Personal Responsibility Education Program, which requires states receiving funding to incorporate lessons about both abstinence and contraception.</p>
<p>In the developing world, programs of reproductive health aimed at teenagers are often small scale and not centrally coordinated, although some countries such as Sri Lanka have a systematic policy framework for teaching about sex within schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-escap_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-escap-42">[42]</a></sup> Non-governmental agencies such as the <a href="/wiki/International_Planned_Parenthood_Federation" title="International Planned Parenthood Federation">International Planned Parenthood Federation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marie_Stopes_International" title="Marie Stopes International">Marie Stopes International</a> provide contraceptive advice for young women worldwide. Laws against <a href="/wiki/Child_marriage" title="Child marriage">child marriage</a> have reduced but not eliminated the practice. Improved female <a href="/wiki/Literacy" title="Literacy">literacy</a> and educational prospects have led to an increase in the age at first birth in areas such as <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, Indonesia, and the Indian state of <a href="/wiki/Kerala" title="Kerala">Kerala</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teenage_pregnancy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Other">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A team of researchers and educators in California have published a list of "best practices" in the prevention of teen pregnancy, which includes, in addition to the previously mentioned concepts, working to "instill a belief in a successful future", male involvement in the prevention process, and designing interventions that are culturally relevant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-joe_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-joe-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevalence">Prevalence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teenage_pregnancy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Prevalence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Prevalence_of_teenage_pregnancy" title="Prevalence of teenage pregnancy">Prevalence of teenage pregnancy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Teenage_birth_rate_per_1000_women_15%E2%80%9319,_2000-09.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="477" data-file-width="940" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Teenage_birth_rate_per_1000_women_15%E2%80%9319%2C_2000-09.svg/260px-Teenage_birth_rate_per_1000_women_15%E2%80%9319%2C_2000-09.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Teenage_birth_rate_per_1000_women_15%E2%80%9319%2C_2000-09.svg/390px-Teenage_birth_rate_per_1000_women_15%E2%80%9319%2C_2000-09.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Teenage_birth_rate_per_1000_women_15%E2%80%9319%2C_2000-09.svg/520px-Teenage_birth_rate_per_1000_women_15%E2%80%9319%2C_2000-09.svg.png 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Teenage_birth_rate_per_1000_women_15%E2%80%9319,_2000-09.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Teenage birth rate per 1,000 females aged 15–19, 2000–2009<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ustats-births_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-ustats-births-97">[97]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Worldwide">Worldwide</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teenage_pregnancy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Worldwide">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In reporting teenage pregnancy rates, the number of pregnancies per 1,000 females aged 15 to 19 when the pregnancy ends is generally used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>Worldwide, teenage pregnancy rates range from 143 per 1000 in some sub-Saharan African countries to 2.9 per 1000 in South Korea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oecd_45-3"><a href="#cite_note-oecd-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ncbi_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-ncbi-99">[99]</a></sup> In the United States, 82% of pregnancies in those between 15 and 19 are unplanned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Among <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developed_countries" title="Developed countries">developed countries</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> have the highest level of teenage pregnancy, while <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a> have the lowest in 2001.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> According to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNFPA" title="UNFPA">UNFPA</a>, “In every region of the world - including high-income countries - girls who are poor, poorly educated or living in rural areas are at greater risk of becoming pregnant than those who are wealthier, well-educated or urban. This is true on a global level, as well: 95 per cent of the world’s births to adolescents (aged 15-19) take place in developing countries. Every year, some 3 million girls in this age bracket resort to unsafe abortions, risking their lives and health.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adolescent_Pregnancy_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-Adolescent_Pregnancy-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to a 2001 <a href="/wiki/UNICEF" title="UNICEF">UNICEF</a> survey, in 10 out of 12 developed nations with available data, more than two thirds of young people have had sexual intercourse while still in their teens. In Denmark, Finland, Germany, Iceland, Norway, the United Kingdom and the United States, the proportion is over 80%. In Australia, the United Kingdom and the United States, approximately 25% of 15-year-olds and 50% of 17-year-olds have had sex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oecd_45-4"><a href="#cite_note-oecd-45">[45]</a></sup> According to <i>The Encyclopedia of Women's Health</i>, published in 2004, approximately 15 million girls under the age of 20 in the world have a child each year. Estimates were that 20–60% of these pregnancies in developing countries are mistimed or unwanted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Teen_Pregnancy_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Teen_Pregnancy-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Save_the_Children" title="Save the Children">Save the Children</a> found that, annually, 13 million children are born to women aged under 20 worldwide, more than 90% in developing countries. <a href="/wiki/Complications_of_pregnancy" title="Complications of pregnancy">Complications of pregnancy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Childbirth" title="Childbirth">childbirth</a> are the leading cause of <a href="/wiki/Death" title="Death">mortality</a> among women aged 15–19 in such areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-savethechildren_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-savethechildren-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sub-Saharan_Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teenage_pregnancy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Sub-Saharan Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The highest rate of teenage pregnancy in the world is in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a>, where women tend to marry at an early age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ncbi_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-ncbi-99">[99]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a>, for example, 87% of women surveyed were married and 53% had given birth to a child before the age of 18.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-subsahara_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-subsahara-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teenage_pregnancy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>, early <a href="/wiki/Marriage" title="Marriage">marriage</a> sometimes results in adolescent pregnancy, particularly in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rural" title="Rural">rural</a> regions where the rate is much higher than it is in <a href="/wiki/Urbanization" title="Urbanization">urbanized</a> areas. Latest data suggests that teen pregnancy in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> is high with 62 pregnant teens out of every 1,000 women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Teen_pregnancies_higher_in_India_than_even_UK.2C_US_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Teen_pregnancies_higher_in_India_than_even_UK.2C_US-102">[102]</a></sup> India is fast approaching to be the most populous country in the world, and increasing teenage pregnancy, an important factor for the population rise, is likely to aggravate the problem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teenage_pregnancy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The rates of early marriage and pregnancy in some Asian countries are high. In recent years, the rates have decreased sharply in <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources.
